In his fantasy, he sees himself as the "catcher in the rye," whose job is to catch any children who might come too close to the edge, thus preventing them from falling off. Here are some of the highlights and key elements of this work:Insight into Roman Emperors: Tacitus provides detailed accounts of the reigns of emperors like Tiberius, Claudius, and Nero. His narrative often focuses on the darker aspects of their rule, including tyranny, corruption, and the abuse of power.The Great Fire of Rome and Nero's Rule: One of the most notable sections deals with the Great Fire of Rome during Nero's reign. Tacitus famously describes Nero's actions during this crisis, including the accusation that Nero sang while Rome burned and his subsequent persecution of Christians as scapegoats.Detailed Descriptions of Military Campaigns: Tacitus gives detailed accounts of various military campaigns and conflicts, such as the Roman conquests in Britain and the battles along the Rhine and Danube frontiers.Political Intrigues and Conspiracies: The work is replete with accounts of political maneuvering, betrayals, and conspiracies within the Roman Senate and among the Roman elite.Year of the Four Emperors: Tacitus vividly describes the chaotic "Year of the Four Emperors" in 69 AD, a tumultuous period that saw the rapid succession of four emperors and significant political upheaval.Moral and Ethical Judgments: Tacitus often interjects his own moral and ethical judgments into his narrative, lamenting the loss of Republican virtues under the imperial system and critiquing the moral decay he perceives in Roman society.Stylistic Brilliance: Tacitus is renowned for his concise, potent, and sometimes enigmatic style. His writing is dense with meaning and often requires careful interpretation.Cultural and Social Insights: Beyond politics and war, Tacitus provides insights into Roman society, culture, and traditions, although always through his own subjective lens."The Histories" is considered an invaluable resource for understanding the Roman Empire, but it's important to note that Tacitus wrote from a specific perspective, often imbued with his personal biases and the political context of his time. "De Agri Cultura," also known as "On Farming" or "On Agriculture," written in 160 BC, is a practical handbook on farming and rural life. Authored by Cato the Elder, it is one of the earliest known works of Roman prose. The work provides a vivid picture of Roman agricultural practices and the rural economy of the time. It covers various topics, including how to cultivate vines, olives, and crops; rear animals; manage slaves; and even make wine. Cato's guide is not just a farming manual; it also reflects his staunch Roman values, emphasizing hard work, thriftiness, and simplicity. The work is significant for its historical and cultural value, offering insights into the daily life and economic practices of early Rome.Seneca's Musings About Living Above a Bathhouse:
In one of his letters (Letter LVI) from "Epistulae Morales ad Lucilium" (Moral Letters to Lucilius), Seneca, the Stoic philosopher, describes his experience of living above a Roman bathhouse. This letter vividly illustrates the noise and chaos he endured daily, from the grunts of weightlifters to the shrillness of a hair plucker and the splashing of water. Seneca uses this experience to discuss the topic of tranquility of the mind. He argues that external disturbances are insignificant to a person who has achieved inner peace. The chaotic bathhouse becomes a metaphor for life's distractions and annoyances. Seneca's point is that a tranquil mind can remain undisturbed and maintain composure regardless of the external environment, demonstrating the Stoic ideal of inner peace and resilience.YouDo you know what Epictetus says about stopping to smell the onions?Nuspeak TranslatorThe reference to "stopping to smell the onions" is not a direct quote from Epictetus, the Stoic philosopher, but rather a humorous reinterpretation or modern twist on the kind of practical wisdom he often imparted. For a horse, qualities like speed, strength, and endurance are beautiful because they fulfill the horse's nature and purpose. A CERTAIN young man a rhetorician came to see Epictetus, with his hair dressed more carefully than was usual and his attire in an ornamental style; whereupon Epictetus said, Tell me if you do not think that some dogs are beautiful and some horses, and so of all other animals. I do think so, the youth replied. Are not then some men also beautiful and others ugly? Certainly. Do we then for the same reason call each of them in the same kind beau- tiful, or each beautiful for something peculiar? And you will judge of this matter thus. Since we see a dog naturally formed for one thing, and a horse for another, and for another still, as an example, a nightingale, we may generally and not improperly declare each of them to be- beautiful then when it is most excellent according to its nature; but since the nature of each is different, each of them seems to me to be beautiful in a different way. Is it not so? He admitted that it was. That then which makes a dog beautiful, makes a horse ugly; and that which makes a horse beautiful, makes a dog ugly, if it is true that their natures are different. It seems to be so. For I think that what makes a Pancratiast beautiful, makes a. wrestler to be not good, and a runner to be most ridiculous; and he who is beautiful for the Pentathlon, is very ugly for wrestling.1 It is so said he. What then makes a man beautiful? Is it that which in its kind makes both a dog and a horse beautiful? It is, he said. What then makes a dog beautiful? The possession of the excellence of a dog. And what makes a horse beautiful? The possession of the excellence of a horse. What then makes a man beautiful? Is it not the possession of the excellence of a man? And do you then, if you wish to be beautiful, young man, labour at this, the acquisition of human excellence. But what is this? Observe whom you yourself praise, when you praise many persons without partiality: do you praise the just or the unjust? The just. Whether do you praise the moderate or the immoderate? The moderate. And the temperate or the intemperate? The temperate. If then you make yourself such a person, you will know that you will make yourself beautiful: but so long as you neglect these things, you must be ugly (Î±á¼°ÏÏÏÏÎ½) even though you contrive all you can to appear beautiful.Nuspeak TranslatorThe passage you've quoted from Epictetus highlights a fundamental Stoic principle regarding the nature of true beauty or excellence. Epictetus, through his dialogue with the young rhetorician, emphasizes that beauty or excellence is determined by the fulfillment of one's natural purpose or function.In this context, he uses the examples of different animals, like dogs and horses, each having its own standard of beauty based on its natural characteristics and purposes. A dog is beautiful when it exhibits the qualities that define the excellence of a dog, such as loyalty or agility, and similarly for a horse, qualities like strength and speed denote beauty.Applying this to humans, Epictetus argues that a person is truly beautiful when they possess human excellence. This excellence isn't about external appearances or adornments but is about embodying virtues like justice, moderation, and temperance. He suggests that striving for moral and virtuous character is what makes a human being truly beautiful.This teaching is a critique of placing too much value on external appearances and superficial attributes. Epictetus urges the young man to focus on developing his character and virtues â the true measure of beauty and excellence in a human being. It's a reminder that the cultivation of inner qualities is far more important than outward adornment, a perspective that resonates strongly with the Stoic philosophyâs emphasis on virtue and character.YouActually that passage was from The Discourses of

By contrasting the physical attributes that define beauty in animals with those that define beauty in humans, he directs the young man's attention (and that of the reader) towards the cultivation of inner virtues.Epictetus's teachings, as presented in "The Discourses," often use practical, everyday situations as starting points for profound ethical and philosophical reflections.
